Jo Jo was a key member of two championship teams, an NBA Finals MVP, a gold medal-winning Olympian, and a Hall of Famer. \u201cHis contributions to the team\u2019s championship legacy may have only been surpassed by the deep and lasting impact that he had in the community. The thoughts and sympathies of the entire Celtics organization are with the White family.\u201d NBA commissioner Adam Silver also issued a statement: White was a key cog in the Celtics return to championship heights in the 1970s, starting for their 1973-74 and 1975-76 title teams while making seven straight All-Star teams. The ninth pick in the 1969 draft out of Kansas, White played 10 seasons with the Celtics before finishing his career with the Warriors and the Kansas City Kings. He retired in 1981, and was selected to the Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Fame in 2015. The Celtics retired his No. 10 in 1982.  In addition to his All-Star selection, White was All-Rookie in 1970 and MVP of the 1976 Finals. He averaged 17.2 points, 4.9 assists and 4.0 rebounds in 832 career appearances. Information from The Associated Press was used in this report. * * *"},{"id":48013,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/warriors-cavaliers-analysis","date":"2018-01-16","title":"Golden State Warriors show off firepower, while Cleveland Cavaliers show flaws in Finals rematch","content":"CLEVELAND \u2014 Tyronn Lue\u2019s bathroom break came early in the fourth quarter. No, not literally. But the coach of the Cleveland Cavaliers had used the familiar call of duty to describe the suddenness with which a game against the Golden State Warriors can turn. And sure enough, on Monday night at Quicken Loans Arena , it turned on Lue and his team. \u201cThey\u2019re the only team,\u201d Lue told reporters before tipoff, \u201cwhere you can be looking at the game and it\u2019s a two-point game. You go to the bathroom, come back, they\u2019re up 15.\u201d Lue\u2019s \u201cloo\u201d moment, figuratively anyway, came after David West sank two free throws to put the Warriors up 95-93 with 9:07 to play. There it was \u2013 the two-point lead \u2013 in what had been 39 minutes of mostly entertaining, back-and-forth, you-loved-them-then-you\u2019ll-love-them-again basketball between the familiar adversaries. Exactly 3 minutes 23 seconds later, Draymond Green cut to the basket, took a pass from Shaun Livingston and dropped in a layup that made it 105-95 . It was the biggest lead of the night to that point. Lue twice had called timeouts during the run in an attempt to stop the bleeding. Now there was only 5:44 left. The Warriors\u2019 margin would grow to 14. And the Cavaliers, to stick with Lue\u2019s imagery, were circling the bowl. That the defending champions can go into hyperdrive against anybody is a reminder, not a revelation. But there were some things revealed, discovered and learned in the second and final regular-season clash of the respective West and East favorites, including: Isaiah Thomas has a way to go. This was our most extended look yet at Cleveland\u2019s new point guard, their Kyrie Irving replacement, in circumstances most like those he\u2019ll face when the meat of the Cavs\u2019 schedule \u2013 the postseason \u2013 rolls around. Thomas scored 19 points, matching his high from the four previous games he played. He was on the floor for 32 minutes, nearly eight minutes more than his previous high. Both Thomas, who missed the season\u2019s first 11 weeks recovering from a hip injury left over from last spring in Boston, and the Cavaliers know a) he\u2019s not sharp or in great shape yet, and b) neither he nor the team has gotten familiar enough with the other to achieve the best results. Yet Thomas took 21 shots Monday, more than LeBron James (18), more than Dwyane Wade (14) and more than double any other Cleveland player. He made just eight, including just one of his seven 3-point attempts. Lue, though, said he had no problem with Thomas\u2019 gunning, as long as they were good shots. Thomas sounded as if he was seeking out work where he could find it. Granted, it was his hip that kept him out but his elbow, wrist and shooting hand apparently profit from heavy usage now too. \u201cI\u2019ve got to get in shape,\u201d he said later. \u201cI\u2019ve got to get my legs back. Especially when I get a little winded, my legs get even heavier. \u201cThe only thing that\u2019s gonna help me is getting reps. Running up and down the floor. Getting my hip, getting my body accustomed to taking a beating. \u2026 Getting in basketball shape.\u201d Kevin Durant didn\u2019t put much stock in Thomas\u2019 play Monday as a sign of how he\u2019ll help Cleveland come springtime. Durant went through a similar enough trial in 2014-15, when surgery in October to repair a Jones fracture in his right foot sidelined him into December, then finally scuttled his season after just 27 games. \u201cObviously IT is just getting back,\u201d the Warriors forward said. \u201cHe hadn\u2019t played in seven months \u2013 you\u2019ve got to give him some time. I know exactly how that feels. Especially being thrown in in the middle of the season and starting and playing 30-plus minutes now \u2026 I know it\u2019s gonna take him a while to get into a comfortable groove here.\u201d What we saw is what we\u2019d get. Mostly. It looked at various points as if both coaches were trying lineups, testing young players, tinkering with substitution patterns or probing matchups with an eye on a possible re-re-rematch in June. Likewise, it would be understandable for Lue and Golden State\u2019s Steve Kerr to hold back a few wrinkles, just to have something fresh to try the next time they face each other. \u201dWe don\u2019t hold too much back, to be honest with you,\u201d Kerr said. \u201cI think matchups can dictate some things that you do in the playoffs and sometimes you may make a few different play calls, whatever. But I don\u2019t think there\u2019s a conscious effort to hold anything back for fear of tipping the hand for later.\u201d Kerr started rookie big Jordan Bell again, same as in the Christmas game in Oakland, for more mobility against Cleveland\u2019s small lineup than center Zaza Pachulia would provide. The coach gave Kevon Looney, Nick Young and Patrick McCaw more tastes of the rivalry too. Lue, meanwhile, was asked if he had the Cavaliers target Steph Curry defensively to get him into foul trouble and generally make life difficult. That\u2019s a tactic that has helped when most others have failed against the two-time MVP and it might come in handy down the road. \u201cI can\u2019t remember,\u201d Lue said, pointedly declining to answer. Curry can dunk.  And David West still can. It was a rare Curry-in-flight moment early in the second half when the Golden State guard, who usually does his damage from deep, threw down a two-handed dunk. It was his first of the season. \u201cI think he was taking out some anger from the first half,\u201d Kerr said. \u201cSometimes that will get him going. Steph loves to dunk more than anybody, you know that. Doesn\u2019t happen often, so when it does, it jacks him up.\u201d Said Durant, who fed Curry for the slam: \u201cHe surprised me on that one. Hopefully he\u2019s feeling better tomorrow. I know it took a lot for him to get up there.\u201d West got his 37-year-old bones up there too, dunking off the dribble during his nine-minute, plus-nine stint in the fourth quarter. The veteran power forward had missed his three shots in the second.  \u201cHe was a little short on his jump shot to start the game,\u201d Durant said. \u201cBut D. West is such a smart player, he makes adjustments, he doesn\u2019t get discouraged. He made a huge play \u2013 his lefthanded dunk kind of got us all going. That was definitely surprising when he turned that one over.\u201d But seriously, Cleveland has issues. Besides losing for the eighth time in 10 games, the Cavaliers had their 13-game home winning streak broken in Monday\u2019s 118-108 loss . And when it was second unit vs. second unit to start the fourth quarter, the Warriors had little trouble switching on defense and crowding away the Cavs\u2019 long-range game. Cleveland shot just 6 of 23 in the fourth quarter , and got outscored 61-44 in what Kerr considered one of his club\u2019s most complete second halves.  The Cavaliers\u2019 defensive leaks have been extensively criticized, and more IT as he gets his game back is not the best way to address those. Even more problematic is the offense now, which over the past few weeks has been grinding, with none of them having much fun with the ball or without. \u201cThis is an important time for us,\u201d Wade said, \u201cand we want to see how we respond coming out of this game. \u2026 It\u2019s not about just winning a ball game. It\u2019s about building good habits as a team.\u201d Given James\u2019 record and reputation \u2013 seven consecutive trips to the Finals, most in spite of some in-season doldrums for his Heat or Cavs crews \u2013 there\u2019s a lot of \u201cIn LeBron We Trust,\u201d both inside and outside the Cleveland locker room. Until a rival in the Eastern Conference proves it can knock off the King, no one will believe it. But if the Cavaliers, after so many exposures to their Bay Area nemeses (9-17 in regular- and postseason meetings since the start of 2014-15), can\u2019t come up with solutions, maybe NBA fans should want to see someone else get a crack at them. If you have an issue with the rankings, or have a question or comment for John Schuhmann, send him an e-mail or contact him via Twitter . * * *"},{"id":42339,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/morning-tip-impact-martin-luther-king-jr-athletes-today-nba","date":"2018-01-15","title":"City of Memphis, NBA navigate carrying out Dr. Martin Luther King Jr.'s message","content":" MEMPHIS \u2014 Swin Cash, a woman I\u2019ve always admired and liked from afar, physically reached out Sunday afternoon. I needed her. I needed someone, for I was no longer functioning. I was standing on the spot where Martin Luther King, Jr., was murdered. I was sobbing. I had taken the tour at the National Civil Rights Museum (NCRM) before, and walked past rooms 306 and 307 at the Lorraine Motel, what\u2019s left of it, anyway, that part of the motel having been preserved by the museum that was built around it in 1991, a jarring conclusion to the tour. What preceded was moving, but mostly known to anyone who has spent any time reading up or studying the Civil Rights movement, the names and events coming all at once: Parks and Faubus; Schwerner, Chaney and Goodman; Leeb, Evers and Connor; Wells and Hamer and Marshall and Eckford. Montgomery. Selma. Lunch Counters. Freedom Summer. But the tour ends by walking past Rooms 307 and 306 of the Lorraine \u2014 the actual rooms, 306 being where King and the Rev. Ralph Abernathy roomed together on April 3, 1968. The bedspreads, the plates, the cigarette butts \u2014 all preserved from that day, frozen for all time, the rooms a shrine-cum-mausoleum of sorts. But I had not been to the museum in many years, and when I had gone on the tour before, the balcony was closed. In 2012, the museum decided to open the balcony to tourgoers. Thus, I was surprised Sunday when a worker at the museum opened the door to the balcony \u2014 the balcony.  It is a small, narrow balcony. There were only a few of us out there, and it was claustrophobic. Such were the accommodations available for black folks in the \u201860s, and King had stayed at the Lorraine many times between 1966 and 1968. You take three steps onto the balcony, and you are \u2026 there. You are standing where King stood when he spoke to Ben Branch, a musician he knew, who was standing beneath King and his coterie in the courtyard. The group was about to go to the home of Rev. Billy Kyles for dinner. \u201cBen, make sure you play \u2018Take My Hand, Precious Lord\u2019 tonight in the meeting,\u201d King asked Branch. \u201cPlay it real pretty.\u201d Seconds later, a rifle shot rang out. You look up. You see where the tour guide says the FBI believes the shot came from \u2014 a window on the top floor of a boarding house just across from the motel. The window is approximately 207 feet from the balcony. It\u2019s not far, not far at all. And you can now understand the damage that one bullet can do fired from so near its target \u2014 King, who stood where I was standing when the .30-06 caliber rifle struck him in the neck, tore through his jaw, severed his spine and lodged in his shoulder. I began to cry. The museum has preserved one square piece of the original concrete from the balcony that was there in 1968. That square once was stained with King\u2019s blood after the shooting; the square has been cleaned and returned, a gravestone with no name on it, its horrible witness to history having changed the world. I began sobbing. And Cash, one of the greatest basketball players ever, and as nice a person as she was great on the court, and who had her infant son in her arms most of the tour, reached out and put her hand on my shoulder. She said nothing.  The dichotomy between the simplicity of what was preserved of April 3 and 4, 1968, in Rooms 306 and 307 of the Lorraine Motel, and the outsized status and stature of the people who toured the museum in 2018 \u2014 almost all people of color \u2014 was jarring. King came to Memphis to fight for economic improvements for poor people, as part of his nationwide Poor People\u2019s Campaign, the last movement he would head. The people on the tour Sunday, former NBA and WNBA stars, assorted Los Angeles Lakers and Memphis Grizziles, the Commissioner of the NBA and the Executive Director of the National Basketball Players Association, me \u2014 all of us are doing ridiculously well financially compared to almost any other person who\u2019s ever lived. The African-American players of the NBA, 300 to 340 or so, are likely the greatest single collection of black wealth and millionaires on earth. What King fought for and died for seems far removed from so many who star in the NBA, who move tens of millions of dollars worth of shoes and sodas and other products, whose faces are recognized around the world. But, of course, that was the whole point of the civil rights movement \u2014 to give black people the ability to rise or fall on their own abilities and talents, and to make the faces of black people something not to be feared or ordered about, but respected and admired. But we were all here, in Memphis, to help commemorate King\u2019s birthday this morning, and to mark the beginning of events in Memphis marking the 50 years that have passed since the assassination. (\u201cAnniversary\u201d does not seem appropriate here.) We were here because the Grizzlies play here every January 15th, the game only a part of days of events and symposiums throughout the city that celebrate King\u2019s life and look to find modern meaning in his legacy. \u201cThis is our opening night, Christmas Day, all wrapped up into one when it comes to the importance of the game to our organization,\u201d Grizzlies President Jason Wexler said Saturday. The Grizzlies have long understood that they must do more than just roll basketballs out on King\u2019s birthday. They have helped create a local charter school \u2014 Grizzlies Prep \u2014 for 5th through 8th grade boys, primarily in low-income neighborhoods and in communities of color. They\u2019ve invested millions of dollars into local mentoring programs. Grizzlies guard Mike Conley pledged $1 million last year toward the Grizzlies\u2019 TEAM Mentor Program. Their efforts in the city made them a finalist last year \u2014 the only NBA team chosen \u2014 for the Sports Humanitarian Team of the Year Award. Former Coach David Fizdale \u2014 who publicly advocated for the removal of statues of Klan founder Nathan Bedford Forrest and Confederacy President Jefferson Davis from local parks ( both were removed last month ) \u2014 worked with local farms that taught people to grow healthier, locally-grown foods to help reduce the obesity numbers in the city. https:\/\/www.youtube.com\/watch?v For years, before their MLK Day game, the team has sponsored a civil rights panel discussion, now officially called the Earl Lloyd Sports Legacy Symposium, in honor of the first black player to play in an NBA game (full disclosure: I\u2019ve hosted it several times) at FedEx Forum. The team has honored former players like Oscar Robertson, Dave Bing and Kareem Abdul-Jabbar over the years who have made significant contributions off the court toward improving the lives of people of color. This year\u2019s 16th Celebration Game will again feature the annual Sports Legacy Award presentation and symposium, which will honor Hall of Famer James Worthy, former players Sam Perkins and Cash, and former player and Memphis native Penny Hardaway. And in Monday\u2019s game, the Grizzlies will debut their City Edition uniforms , which come with a wonderful and historic touch: the \u201cMEMPHIS\u201d font on the front of their jerseys is the same font that was used for the iconic \u201cI AM A MAN\u201d posters and placards that sanitation workers in the city used after they went on strike in the spring of 1968 \u2014 the event that brought Dr. King to Memphis in his last days. \u201cWe\u2019re acutely aware of our history in this city,\u201d Wexler said. \u201cFedEx Forum, to me, is the greatest address in sports. Our address is 191 Beale Street, and our boundaries are Beale Street ( where the blues took hold as an iconic American music institution), B.B. King Boulevard, Martin Luther King Avenue and Church park, named for Robert Church, who was the first African-American millionaire, post-Civil War. And across the street from us is Clayborn Temple.\u201d Clayborn Temple is where the sanitation workers produced and handed out the I AM A MAN placards. Wexler, John Pugliese, the team\u2019s vice president of marketing, communications and broadcast, and Director of Creative Services David Thompson worked with Nike over the last year-plus on the uniform design. (Nike, which designed every team\u2019s City Edition uniforms, also has a large presence in Memphis; its $300 million North American Logistics Campus here is the largest single entity for the company outside Nike\u2019s home state of Oregon.) They consulted with the NCRM.  \u201cThis was not a moment where you wanted splashes of color and something that stood out,\u201d Wexler said. \u201cIt had to be dignified and respectful of the moment. These great black and white photos, people marching in their suits \u2026 it just all came together. You\u2019re not going to stick a neon color on something like that. That\u2019s not what this moment is about.\u201d But what is it about? NBA players engage in wonderful acts of philanthropy, service and dialogue with the community in every NBA city. No sentient person doubts LeBron James\u2019 sincerity in pledging college scholarships for every public school kid in his native Akron, Ohio, through a partnership with the University of Akron. No one would argue that DeMarcus Cousins wasn\u2019t genuine when he brought cops and citizens of Sacramento and Birmingham together in separate events over the last year. But do they work in the spirit of what King preached? \u201cOf course, the struggles that the activist athletes of today are waging is in the truest and most profound spirit and vision of Dr. King \u2014 struggle founded upon a concern over injustice perpetrated against the vulnerable and voiceless, a courageous commitment to address and confront that injustice boldly, nonviolently, and uncompromisingly, and a willingness to sacrifice their fortunes, risk their careers and (if the death threats so many of them have received are factored in) their very lives in their pursuit of \u2018that more Perfect Union,\u2019 \u201d said Dr. Harry Edwards, the prominent sociologist and civil rights advocate, via e-mail. \u201cWere Dr. M.L. King here in body with us today, it would be my guess that he would be \u2018taking a knee with (Colin) Kaepernick, raising a fist with Malcolm Jenkins, taking a seat with Marshawn Lynch, or simply saying AMEN! to the remarks of LeBron, D-Wade, CP3, and Carmelo at the ESPYs a couple of years back,\u201d Edwards wrote. Yet despite the presence of a mammoth corporation like FedEx, which is headquartered here, and other huge companies like Auto Zone, International Paper and ServiceMaster, Memphis, and especially its black citizens, remain poor \u2014 ear-splittingly poor. The \u201cMemphis Poverty Fact Sheet\u201d for 2017 , published last September by the University of Memphis, ticked off the numbers: the city\u2019s poverty rate is 26.9 percent. Child poverty in the city is \u2026 44.7 percent. The poverty rate for non-Hispanic African-Americans is 32.3 percent. Memphis is the poorest Metropolitan Statistical Area (MSA) in the nation with more than a million people. So, all the great work that the Grizzlies do in the community \u2026 what does it mean? Does it have impact? Does any of this have any impact? And is that too much to ask of any one NBA player, or team, or league, for that matter? These aren\u2019t mathematicians and social scientists; these are guards and forwards and marketers. (And, lawyers. Lots and lots of lawyers.) Memphis thus strains under the dual one-two punch it gets, for its past and its present. \u201cThere\u2019s always this narrative that Memphis is still, like, staggering, really, under the shame of being the city where King was killed, even 50 years out,\u201d said Wendi Thomas, a journalist and 2016 Neiman Fellow who launched MLK 50: Justice Through Journalism , an ongoing nonprofit online reporting project highlighting the stark economic problems that remain in Memphis, and is supported by the National Civil Rights Museum in conjunction with the museum\u2019s MLK50 commemoration. \u201cWe\u2019re in this constant process of atonement,\u201d Thomas said. \u201cI think part of the challenge becomes, to you, what part of King\u2019s legacy are you trying to redeem? If it\u2019s just the little black boys and little white boys holding hands part, I think the Grizzlies as an organization do a good job of bringing diverse groups of people together under the same roof. If you understand why King was in Memphis \u2014 which is about jobs and wages, underpaid public employees \u2014 then the question could be, do the Grizzlies even have a place in that, in that discussion? Because they\u2019re a business. But I would argue that, yes they do, and moreover, so does any other business or entity that\u2019s going to trot out a King quote on that day.\u201d Indeed, King\u2019s pivot from demanding civil rights for African-Americans to fighting for economic justice is still, to this day, far less discussed and celebrated by most. In 1966, King went to Chicago to fight against segregated housing, but also to demand an increase in the local minimum wage, to $2 an hour \u2014 in today\u2019s dollars, $15.13 per hour \u2014 just exactly about what the \u201cFight for $15\u201d movement is seeking across the country today. And King did not fare as well and have the universal support for his economic fights than he did for civil rights; he famously said, after being hit in the head by a rock during a march in Marquette Park in August, 1966, \u201cI think the people from Mississippi ought to come to Chicago to learn how to hate.\u201d The city\u2019s notorious Mayor, Richard J. Daley, placated King, but never followed through on the promises he made the minister while King was there. Nonetheless, King came to Memphis after sanitation workers Echol Cole and Robert Walker were killed on Feb. 1, 1968 , when a malfunctioning garbage truck shorted out, starting the truck\u2019s compactor while the men were on the back of the truck. They couldn\u2019t escape in time and were crushed inside. Soon after their deaths, on Feb. 12, 1968, 1,300 of Memphis\u2019s sanitation workers went on strike, demanding not only improved working conditions ( the \u201cwiener barrel\u201d trucks like the one that malfunctioned were horribly outdated and often flipped over as well), but modern-day financial amenities. They had \u201cno benefits, no pension, no overtime, no grievance procedure, no insurance, no uniforms\u201d \u2026 . King had announced in December, 1967, that he was starting a nationwide moment \u2014 a \u201cPoor People\u2019s Campaign\u201d \u2014 to highlight the stark poverty that still roiled large swaths of cities and rural areas around the country. The campaign demanded $30 billion in annual federal money to combat poverty, construction of 500,000 low-cost homes and Congressional backing for a full employment, guaranteed wage bill. Plans were made for a second March on Washington, similar to the seminal one in 1963, the site of King\u2019s \u201cI Have a Dream\u201d speech , which would feature the construction of a shanty town on the grounds of the National Mall where protestors would remain until the proposed bills were passed into law. At the invitation of his longtime friend, the Rev. James Lawson, who had been helping lead the protests in Memphis, King came to the city in March to offer his public support for the striking workers. Police had shot mace into protesting crowds in late February; more than 100 people were arrested in early March. King arrived on March 18 and spoke to more than 10,000 people at the Mason Temple; he pledged to return later in the month and lead a march himself. He came back on March 28 for the march, but a few rioters who broke windows as the march went down Beale Street made finishing the march peacefully impossible. At the urging of fellow clergy, who feared for his safety, King left the march before police descended on the protestors. One teenager, thought to be looting, was shot and killed by police, and hundreds of people were arrested. It was one of the first times a march led by King had fallen into violence, in part, by his own protestors. He had prior commitments out of town in the next days, but vowed he would again return to Memphis to lead another march. He returned to Memphis on April 3, 1968, and checked into Room 306 of the Lorraine Motel. Speaking again at Mason Temple, King delivered the last speech of his life. He was not feeling well, yet roused himself, as ever, to bring his crowd to its feet. The ending of the speech, of course, is what almost everyone remembers; almost prophetically, King said that while he\u2019d like to live a long life \u2014 \u201clongevity has its place,\u201d he said \u2014 what happened to his physical body no longer mattered to him. https:\/\/www.youtube.com\/watch?v \u201cI just want to do God\u2019s will,\u201d he said . \u201cAnd he\u2019s allowed me to go up to the mountain. And I\u2019ve looked over. And I\u2019ve seen \u2026 the promised land. I may not get there with you. But I want you to know tonight, that we as a people will get to the promised land! So I\u2019m happy tonight \u2014 I\u2019m not worried about anything; I\u2019m not fearing any man! Mine eyes have seen the glory of the coming of the Lord!\u201d Yet the bulk of King\u2019s speech was not about prophecy, but policy. \u201cThe issue is the refusal of Memphis to be fair and honest in its dealings with its public servants, who happen to be sanitation workers,\u201d he said earlier in the speech \u2014 which ran more than 40 minutes, not three . \u201cNow we\u2019ve got to keep attention on that \u2026 we just need to go around to these stores, and to these massive industries in our country, and say, \u2018God sent us by here to say to you that you\u2019re not treating His children right. And we\u2019ve come by here to ask you to make the first item on your agenda fair treatment where God\u2019s children are concerned. Now if you are not prepared to do that, we do have an agenda that we must follow. And our agenda calls for withdrawing economic support from you.\u201d King called for Memphis\u2019 black citizens not to buy Coca-Cola, or Sealtest Milk. He asked they boycott not one, but two bread companies \u2014 Wonder Bread and Hart\u2019s Bread. \u201cWe are choosing these companies because they haven\u2019t been fair in their hiring policies,\u201d King said, \u201cand we are choosing them because they can begin the process of saying they are going to support the needs and the rights of these men who are on strike. And then they can move on downtown and tell Mayor (Henry) Loeb to do what is right.\u201d He told Memphis\u2019 black population to take its money out of white banks and to patronize Tri-State Bank, a black-owned bank founded in 1946; to do business with black insurance companies; to \u201cbegin the process of building a greater economic base, and at the same time, we are putting pressure where it really hurts,\u201d King said. So, where does that leave Memphis, and the people who live and work here now? \u201cI think today, to honor King, you\u2019ve got to look at what you pay your workers,\u201d Thomas said. \u201cDon\u2019t put a King quote up on the screen, on the JumboTron \u2014 that new, beautiful JumboTron \u2014 but your workers are making $9 an hour, talking about, get your beer here. Then, you\u2019re just playing. It feels like you\u2019re mocking what King stood for. And that bothers me, as somebody who started this non-profit journalism project, who raised a lot of money so we can make sure we can pay our workers at least $15 an hour, all our contributors. Is it this team\u2019s job to lead that conversation? I\u2019m not sure. But I know that\u2019s where Coach Fizdale was headed.\u201d Politically, at least, there have been stirrings in the city among the city\u2019s black residents, with the statue removal serving as a catalyst. \u201cThe woman who really led the \u2018Take \u2018Em Down\u2019 movement, Tami Sawyer , she\u2019s also running for office (Shelby County Commission),\u201d Thomas said. \u201cIt seems like every other day, I see on Facebook, some announcement, some young black person, who\u2019s announced that they\u2019re running for a local office in Memphis. And what I think it\u2019s doing is, it\u2019s creating this new wave of young black people who think they can change their world, too. And that, to me, is exciting.\u201d It is beyond any one player, no matter how famous or rich; beyond any one league, no matter how popular or viewed. Yet so many come here to Memphis, this year, to vow that they have not forgotten who came before, and to, as John Wesley said, Do all the good you can, By all the means you can, In all the ways you can, In all the places you can, At all the times you can, To all the people you can, As long as ever you can. \u201cWill these activist athletes words and deeds \u2018make a difference?,\u2019 \u201d Edwards asked. \u201cAnytime people stand up based upon a principled commitment to freedom and justice, THAT IS THE IMPACT! When the words and actions of activist athletes precipitate comments from presidents, imitation across the spectrum of the sports hierarchy from Pop Warner football to women\u2019s soccer, when there are entire departments of public defenders (the Alameda Co. Office) go public with a group photo taking a knee, when Congressmen and women are taking a knee on the floor of the US Congress, when 2-year old begs to wear his Kaepernick jersey so that he can take a knee \u2014 has the athlete activist movement had an impact ? Yes \u2014 I\u2019d say so! The views on this page do not necessarily reflect the views of the NBA, its clubs or Turner Broadcasting."},{"id":42353,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/utah-jazz-donovan-mitchell-enjoying-surprising-star-turn-rookie-season","date":"2018-01-15","title":"Rookie Donovan Mitchell surprises many -- including himself -- with his star turn","content":" Donovan Mitchell swears, to this day, that he didn\u2019t think would be a first-round pick. \u201cI didn\u2019t think I was going to be drafted, to be honest,\u201d he said last week. \u201cI\u2019m so serious. I mean, wholeheartedly, too. I don\u2019t know. I didn\u2019t realize it, probably, until about a week and a half, two weeks before the Draft. Once I got invited to the Green Room, that\u2019s when I was like, okay, I\u2019m first, I\u2019m getting drafted in the first round. Because they try their best not to let guys sit in there forever. \u201cSo I\u2019m like, first round. Now, will I be drafted to the Knicks at eight, or all the way down to, who was it? OKC or Utah. \u2018Cause OKC had 22 and Utah had 24. So now that was a factor. From before then, I didn\u2019t know what to expect, to be honest.\u201d If he was surprised in June, when he was taken 13th overall by the Denver Nuggets \u2014 and then minutes later traded to Utah \u2014 it\u2019s the rest of the league that\u2019s been shocked since. Mitchell had a lot of supporters before the Draft, including Utah, which traded up from 24 to 13 in the first round by sending former first-rounder Trey Lyles and the No. 24 pick in 2017, Tyler Lydon, to Denver . But even after looking like the best player in the Las Vegas Summer League, no one, including Utah, thought Mitchell would streak across the sky this quickly. To date, Mitchell leads all rookies (and the Jazz) in scoring (18.8 per game) , having worked himself into the starting lineup before Thanksgiving. He broke the franchise\u2019s single-game rookie scoring record (41 points) well before Christmas, and helped lead his squad to a win over LeBron James and the Cavs on Dec. 30 , leaving James to compare Mitchell to a young Dwyane Wade. Highlight-reel dunks have been a regular occurrence at Vivint Smart Home Arena. He\u2019s given the Blazers 28 , Minnesota and Milwaukee 24 . Oklahoma City got 31 ; Houston, 26 . After shooting 41.3 percent in November , he shot 50.7 percent in December . \u201cI\u2019ve played with a lot of good rookies, but this kid is special,\u201d guard Ricky Rubio said of Mitchell. \u201cJust because he\u2019s willing to learn. He\u2019s asking a lot of questions. He\u2019s humble. And he keeps improving. He\u2019s improved so fast. He watches film all the time, he asks questions, and he\u2019s willing to learn. Most of them are good questions. But he knows the game and you can tell, because he knows the game very well and he wants to get better.\u201d His arrival, coming just as Gordon Hayward was leaving town to go to Boston via free agency , was a much-needed pick-me-up for a franchise that had been gut punched. You don\u2019t lose your franchise player for nothing without it hurting \u2014 and, make no mistake, it hurt. The Jazz did everything it could to keep Hayward happy, methodically building a strong team around him, making development a priority. Utah beat the LA Clippers in the first round of the 2017 playoffs with Hayward; without him, and with numerous injuries to its core group, including separate, long-term injuries to Utah\u2019s irreplaceable center, Rudy Gobert, the Jazz is stumbling along, off the pace for a playoff spot and in 10th place in the Western Conference. \u201cI think we knew going into the year \u2014 the word we\u2019ve used is \u2018pivot\u2019 \u2014 what that looks like, how that takes place,\u201d Jazz coach Quin Snyder said. \u201cWe\u2019re kind of open to, we\u2019re all a little curious about how that would evolve. It hasn\u2019t evolved, in some ways, with Rudy being out. In other ways, it\u2019s evolved, it\u2019s just been different. We\u2019re still kind of gathering information about our team. But I think in Donovan\u2019s case, what he\u2019s been able to do, he\u2019s been a catalyst for a lot of things. He\u2019s been a catalyst for his teammates. We\u2019ve seen certain guys find roles that I think they\u2019re going to be successful in. In some cases, we\u2019re not able to do that, because of Rudy. It\u2019s really kind of two things that are happening at once.\u201d Mitchell was initially projected to go in the late teens or early 20s in the first round, after declaring following his sophomore season at Louisville. His pedigree was at once enticing and troubling \u2014 \u201cRick (Pitino, the former Cardinals\u2019 coach) makes his players better than they are,\u201d one team executive said before the Draft. But Mitchell started rising team boards following a series of excellent pre-Draft workouts. The Jazz believed Mitchell would be a good fit in their system, but also knew it would have to move up to get him, and Utah could only get so high in the first round. But once Mitchell got past Charlotte at 10 (the Hornets took Malik Monk) and Detroit at 11 (the Pistons took Luke Kennard), Jazz General Manager Dennis Lindsey made the move. \u201cWhen we were able to draft him, I know how enthusiastic Dennis was going into that,\u201d Snyder said. \u201cHe\u2019s been a catalyst for a lot of growth, and I think the results speak for themselves. But I think it\u2019s also, the best thing about Donovan is, similar to the rest of our group, there\u2019s a hunger on his part to improve and get better. That enthusiasm and that kind of youthful exuberance is a good thing for our team.\u201d Snyder has been convinced, to the point where he\u2019s now running plays for Mitchell at the ends of games. Sometimes it works, as it did against Cleveland; sometimes it doesn\u2019t, as when Mitchell missed against Miami, and Josh Richardson hit the game-winner for the Heat instead . \u201cThat was part of the moment,\u201d Mitchell said. \u201cI\u2019ve always been one to kind of, I\u2019ve always wanted to be the guy. I watched, my favorite player growing up was LeBron. One thing I noticed with him was, it\u2019s always on him at the end of the game. I\u2019m not saying that\u2019s the case now, but I\u2019ve always had that mentality of not being afraid of the moment \u2014 made shot, missed shot. I made one against Cleveland and I missed one in Miami that should have been a pass that I didn\u2019t make. It comes with the territory. You\u2019ve got to keep learning. But that was a pretty cool moment.\u201d But he messed up a few nights later in Miami. \u201cRodney (Hood) had it going in the fourth quarter, and for me to miss him, it\u2019s definitely one thing that I\u2019m still kicking myself for,\u201d Mitchell said. \u201cAfter the game I went to him and said \u2018look, it\u2019s my fault. You were wide open. That\u2019s on me.\u2019 And that goes back to what I said about having it be on your shoulders, and just having that mentality. That\u2019s how I\u2019ve always been.\u201d The Jazz acquired Rubio from Minnesota to play the point and to try and convince Hayward to stay. But Mitchell\u2019s versatility has allowed Snyder to put him on the ball for stretches already, making his ultimate NBA position a mystery. Utah has a lot of options in its backcourt, and it hasn\u2019t given up on former Lottery pick Dante Exum, who\u2019s likely to miss the entire season after suffering a shoulder injury in preseason . But Mitchell makes Exum\u2019s absence survivable \u2014 and, maybe, his future in Salt Lake City uncertain. \u201cHe\u2019s bringing it every night, and he wants it more,\u201d Rubio said. \u201cWe know how tough it is your rookie season; imagine if you\u2019re one of the top guys on the team, and we expect you to bring it every night, because you showed you can do it. The (expectation) level is rising higher and higher every day, just because of the way he does things.\u201d Rubio had immense pressure on him when Minnesota famously took him fifth in the 2009 Draft , a teenager from Spain who\u2019d already had all the world\u2019s attention on him for years. He knows what it\u2019s like to be a guard expected to raise a team to the next level. \u201cThere are a lot of rookies that are still young, but they\u2019re young off the court,\u201d Rubio said of Mitchell. \u201cHe\u2019s really, he\u2019s a kid, but he knows what is going on.\u201d The Jazz could ultimately run some two-guard fronts with Mitchell and Rubio, maybe Mitchell and Exum, though one of those two would have to become a bigger perimeter threat. Mitchell, a former star high school baseball player \u2014 his father, Donovan, Sr., was a former minor leaguer who\u2019s now an executive with the New York Mets \u2014 has taken to Snyder as quickly as he bonded with Pitino, with whom he stays in touch despite Pitino\u2019s ouster from Louisville in November. \u201cCoach (Snyder) and I, the good thing is, we have a relationship where, he\u2019ll call a play, call a play for me, and I\u2019m like \u2018no, coach, Joe (Johnson) just hit two. Let\u2019s get Joe a third one,\u2019 \u201d Mitchell said. \u201cAnd he\u2019s like, he\u2019s not offended when I speak up. We have a relationship where we can talk back and forth. There\u2019s no disrespect. It\u2019s just, I give my opinion, and he listens. Sometimes, he says no. It\u2019s because he\u2019s been in the game a lot longer than I have, obviously. But it\u2019s just the fact that he allows me to give my input in, and allows me to learn that way, that\u2019s awesome.\u201d The injuries \u2014 Hood and Derrick Favors and Johnson have all missed time this season, along with Gobert, who\u2019s only played 18 games \u2014 have made continuity impossible. (It\u2019s only getting worse; guard Thabo Sefolosha is likely out for the season with a knee injury suffered Friday against Charlotte.) That Utah had to not only replace Hayward, but opted not to bring back Boris Diaw and George Hill, bringing in veteran free agents Jonas Jerebko, Sefolosha and Ekpe Udoh \u2014 who came from Europe, where he was MVP of EuroLeague last spring \u2014 has made everyone\u2019s adjustments harder. Jerebko has had to play center of late. \u201cWe\u2019ve got a lot of new players,\u201d Jerebko said. \u201cRicky, obviously, the leader of the team is new, too. So it\u2019s not like we\u2019re going to, right away, start clicking. Obviously, it\u2019s going to take some time. But we\u2019ve got some great guys on this team who are willing to learn, share the ball, unselfish players. We\u2019re going to figure it out. \u201c Mitchell has played with Rubio, Hood and Joe Ingles on the ball at times, depending on lineups and situations. Like all rookies, he\u2019s learning what the right reads and cuts are, making sure the right guy gets the right shot at the right time. Sometimes, that guy is him. Defense, and getting through screens, provide a nightly challenge. \u201cIt was pretty easy in college, because you always either run through a guy or you find a way to get around him,\u201d Mitchell said. \u201cBut right now, there\u2019s no running through DeMarcus Cousins. There\u2019s no running through those guys. So you\u2019ve just got to be able to find a way, and understand you\u2019re going to get hit. You\u2019ve got to be able to pursue and follow. Playing for Coach Pitino, Coach Pitino said you should never be hit by any screen. So that\u2019s my mentality, and I get pretty down on myself when I get hit by screens. But I\u2019m starting to undersand that that\u2019s inevitable; it\u2019s going to happen. Making guys work for their shots. If a guy has 40, make him work for the 40. \u2018Cause guys are good enough to have 40, and there\u2019s nothing you can do about it. But make sure they work their hardest to get to it.\u201d Keeping a similar routine is important for Mitchell. At Louisville, he took the same number of shots before a game, sang the same song as he walked into every arena. (\u201cThat\u2019s just how superstitious I am. I can\u2019t say [what the song is]; that would mess everything up,\u201d he says.) In Utah, he has Caesar salad from Cheesecake Factory for lunch and dinner. Mitchell and Utah\u2019s other young players were in Vegas when Hayward announced he was leaving. The next few weeks were tough ones for one of the NBA\u2019s proudest franchises. But Mitchell gives Utah hope. Every team needs hope, but a small market franchise is especially needy. And if Mitchell becomes a flashy, exciting star guard, Utah\u2019s ability to get and retain other talented players in the future to play with him and Gobert increases dramatically. But Mitchell says he\u2019s just doing what needs to be done. The talk of him becoming Utah\u2019s next superstar player is not on his radar. \u201cWhen Dante got hurt, I had to play more minutes at point guard,\u201d Mitchell said. \u201cThen Rodney got hurt, so I\u2019m playing all these minutes. I\u2019ve got to learn these players, from one, two and three, and as the point guard you\u2019ve got to know all five spots. So I\u2019m in there watching film tremendously. But it never crossed my mind to replace (Hayward). That\u2019s not who I am. That\u2019s not where I came from. I want to be the best teammate I can be, the fastest learner, the hardest worker, and just go out there in any way that I can help in each game. If I\u2019m having an off night, make sure the guys who are having an on night get the ball. Rebounding, playing defense. If I\u2019m having an on night, continue to score and making the right plays, whatever it may be.\u201d Utah has a lot of decisions to make in the coming months about the direction of the franchise. Favors will be an unrestricted free agent at season\u2019s end; Exum and Hood will be restricted. Sefolosha, Udoh and Jerebko are all on non-guaranteed deals for 2018-19. But one piece is solidly in place for a long, long time \u2014 one who looks like he\u2019ll be must-see TV most every night, and a threat to make the highlights every game. \u201cI think the craziest thing is not so much watching myself, it\u2019s watching who\u2019s guarding me, or who I\u2019m guarding,\u201d Mitchell said. \u201cThat\u2019s the biggest thing. I watch film religiously, on the flight or wherever it may be. I\u2019m watching, and I\u2019m like, wow, I got a stop \u2014 in the game, I won\u2019t think it \u2014 but I\u2019m like, \u2018wow, I just got a stop on \u2018Bron. I got a stop on Westbrook.\u2019 Those are the things that kind of take me back for a second. Not so much that I\u2019m making those plays, but it\u2019s like, last year, I was watching this guy against the Warriors in the Finals, and screaming and yelling at the TV. Now it\u2019s like in the late moments, making a layup like that (against James\u2019 Cavaliers), it\u2019s like, it\u2019s crazy how it all happened.\u201d * * * Longtime NBA reporter, columnist and Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Famer David Aldridge is an analyst for TNT. They both covet what the other wants \u2014 in fact, the Warriors or the Cavs could make a persuasive case that, if not for the other guys, one already would have notched a three-peat and be chasing Bill Russell\u2019s Celtics in pursuit of a fourth consecutive championship. They both have poured buckets of blood, sweat, tears, money, Gatorade and offseason counter moves into their nouveau NBA rivalry. And they both, well, as Golden State coach Steve Kerr phrased it to the San Jose Mercury News Sunday, \u201cWe just want to kick each other\u2019s ass.\u201d And yet the Warriors and the Cavaliers \u2014 who play again Monday at Quicken Loans Arena in the NBA\u2019s prime-time MLK showdown \u2014 have more in common with each other than they do with any of the league\u2019s other 28 teams. Playing 100 games or so every year. Locking in mentally and surviving physically longer than anyone else. Showing up each night targeted as a measuring stick, even a season maker, by the opponents. While trying like heck to keep things fresh. Renew. Find and tap into a new source of energy, because old ones wane over time. \u201cIt\u2019s the biggest challenge of this whole season,\u201d Kerr told NBA.com late last week, with the Warriors starting a back-to-back in Milwaukee and Toronto on their way back to The Land. Even if it were possible \u2014 and it realistically is not, given free agency, injuries, trades, the salary cap, luxury taxes, hirings and firings each NBA offseason \u2014 playing a pat hand from one championship-level season to the next isn\u2019t desirable. Voices, locker rooms, relationships get stale. Rivals adjust and escalate in the arms race. Some players ebb in the pecking order, others flow. It\u2019s important to inject new faces, add skills and even find fresh themes to fend off monotony, even boredom, through the 82-game slogs. The Warriors, in winning 20 of 23 games over the past seven weeks, largely have managed to do that. The Cavaliers, at 26-15 after 2-7 stretch that started at Golden State on Christmas? Not so much. It\u2019s easy to think of Golden State\u2019s success since Kerr\u2019s hiring before the 2014-15 season as one uninterrupted run of excellence. Three-pointers, \u201cdeath lineups,\u201d and the rest. But the differences from one year to the next have been fairly pronounced. \u201cIn Year 1, we were trying to prove ourselves to the world,\u201d Kerr said. \u201cThen we win the championship \u2014 it was all so fresh. There were no letdowns at all that year. It was the most exciting, it was the most energized, it was the most refreshing. It was brand new to all of us. It felt like we were riding this wave all year \u2014 we were all giddy, like, \u2018Oh my God, we\u2019re really good!\u2019 We didn\u2019t know we could be like that. And for me, it was my first year coaching.\u201d Stephen Curry won his first MVP award . He and Klay Thompson generated considerable conversation about the best shooting backcourts in league history. Draymond Green forever changed the old NBA notion of \u201c\u2019tweeners.\u201d The Warriors finished 67-15, ranked second in the league in offense (111.6) and first in defense (101.4) and beat Cleveland in the Finals in six games. \u201cIt was maybe like the first stages when you fall in love,\u201d Kerr said. \u201cYou\u2019re just on Cloud 9 and she can\u2019t do anything wrong. There\u2019s infatuation and then you truly fall in love, and it\u2019s amazing. \u201cThe second year, we sort of rode that wave of euphoria of being the best team in the league and having won the title. The next thing you know, we\u2019re 24-0 and we\u2019ve got a chance to set an all-time record. That 73-win mark carried us all year. We were going to prove that, not only were we the champs but we were one of the best teams ever.\u201d The Warriors were \u2014 by regular season standards. Curry won his second MVP award. Kerr missed the first 43 games due to health issues but assistant coach Luke Walton steered them to a 39-4 mark. They bought into the chase for 73 victories fairly late, but instead of a 16-5 playoff run like the previous spring\u2019s, the Warriors went 15-9 \u2014 coming up one victory short when the Cavaliers became the first team to claw back from a 3-1 deficit . That led directly to Golden State\u2019s next new wrinkle, a reconfiguration that came close to buckling the league\u2019s knees. \u201cWe got KD,\u201d Kerr said. \u201cNow we\u2019re changing our team, right? Last year was about incorporating KD, welcoming this incredible player into our organization and our roster. Figuring how to do it, how we were going to adjust. I felt like there were times last year that were tiring, where our guys were done a little bit. But it was \u2018new\u2019 again.\u201d Even the challenges were fresh, like counting Curry\u2019s or Klay Thompson\u2019s touches relative to Durant\u2019s or closing ranks around Golden State\u2019s thin man as his reputation took blows for the first time in his NBA career. Not interested in shooting for 74 victories, the Warriors simply took care of business and stayed coiled for the postseason. Then it was a 16-1 dash to title No. 2, Durant snagging the Finals MVP trophy after the five-game dispatching of the Cavs . All of which just set the Warriors\u2019 bar higher, requiring them to search for something new, somebody borrowed, presumably nothing blue. \u201cThis year it\u2019s just survive and advance,\u201d Kerr said. \u201cIt\u2019s \u2018let\u2019s get to April, May, June in one piece.\u2019 There\u2019s a reason we\u2019ve lost six home games already. We don\u2019t have the driving force that we had the last few years. We\u2019re dealing with what any team in NBA history that\u2019s tried to do this has dealt with. The Lakers (1982-85), the Celtics (1984-87, 1957-66)\u2026 It\u2019s just really hard and you need that driving force.\u201d Said Warriors vet Andre Iguodala: \u201cYour body is mindful of it, because it hurts.\u201d A couple of young guys \u2014 Patrick McCaw, Kevon Looney \u2014 have taken on bigger roles. Nick Young brings some sort of buzz into any locker room that will have him. Still, as veteran guard Shaun Livingston said: \u201cWe\u2019re not chasing any records. We\u2019re not adding another All Star. We\u2019re just trying to make it through the marathon.\u201d The Cavaliers are just trying to make it through the marathon, too. But if they could, they might do it like Rosie Ruiz, the 1980 women\u2019s \u201cwinner\u201d of the Boston Marathon who perpetrated a hoax by hopping the subway and running only the final mile of Beantown\u2019s famous race. The 2017-18 has been anything but fun for Cleveland so far. It began with the departure of All-Star point guard Kyrie Irving, a not-so-funhouse mirror image of Durant\u2019s arrival a year earlier in the Bay Area. Irving, for reasons still not quite explained, made it known in the offseason that he wanted out. He wanted to be the man on his own team. Or he didn\u2019t want to be left in the lurch if (when?) LeBron James took his talents elsewhere again. Or both. Or neither. Regardless, once the Cavaliers made his request come true by dealing him to Boston for All-Star point guard Isaiah Thomas and Brooklyn\u2019s coveted first-round pick this June, their task got tougher and their season longer. Losing one of the league\u2019s best ball handlers and shot makers doesn\u2019t qualify as \u201crenewal\u201d any more than what went on in Oklahoma City when Durant packed up. There\u2019s been more. Shooting guard J.R. Smith seemingly got old overnight. Jae Crowder, who came from the Celtics in the Irving deal, hasn\u2019t meshed with the Cavs\u2019 style. Kevin Love has been moved to center but hasn\u2019t done anything to satisfy the Cavs\u2019 need for rim protection. Thomas only returned to action from a hip injury as the calendar turned to 2018 and has played only four games in these two weeks. Even with so many new faces \u2014 seven of the top 12 in coach Tyronn Lue\u2019s rotation weren\u2019t here 12 months ago \u2014 it\u2019s a group heavy on veterans, players a little too established or mature to naturally instill raw energy. James said recently that none of this is new, it\u2019s another case of the Cavs biding their time for the \u201csecond\u201d season that means everything. But Lue also introduced the topic of \u201cagendas,\u201d suggesting that some of his guys were looking out for their own responsibilities and performances \u2014 particularly on defense \u2014 rather than the group\u2019s. At best, this is another dose of the midseason blahs, the Cavs in their doldrums in need of an All-Star break. At worst, though, they might be honing some bad habits that won\u2019t be so easy to break in May or June. Especially if East rivals such as Toronto, Boston or Washington are emboldened after witnessing or administering some of the Cavs\u2019 more embarrassing beat downs this season. Will any of this matter come spring? It will if the switch each team is minding stubbornly decides not to flip. \u201cThat\u2019s the key. You\u2019ve got to find that balance,\u201d Kerr said. \u201cAre you flipping the switch or are you navigating? The idea is, don\u2019t let bad habits slip in. Right now, this moment, we\u2019re into some bad habits. Our defensive efforts the last five, six games [before the weekend] were awful. We got away with it because Steph was going nuts.\u201d The Cavaliers repeatedly have not gotten away with bad defensive habits, even on nights when James has been dominant. \u201cIt\u2019s tough,\u201d Livingston said. \u201cThey\u2019re a team that\u2019s built for the playoffs. But our core guys still are in there prime. Their core guys are still good. But we\u2019re talking about \u2018prime.\u2019\u201d Most still would pick both Golden State and Cleveland to advance all the way to a \u201cFinals Four\u201d (after last year\u2019s \u201cRubber Match\u201d series). But one of these years, most will be wrong \u2014 about one or both. That alone might be motivation enough. Steve Aschburner has written about the NBA since 1980. Fans account for 50 percent of the vote to determine the 10 starters for the 67th NBA All-Star Game , which will take place on Sunday, Feb. 18 at Staples Center in Los Angeles (8 p.m. ET\/5 p.m. PT, TNT). All current NBA players and a panel of basketball media account for 25 percent each, with every participant completing one full ballot featuring two guards and three frontcourt players from each conference. Voting for fans, players and media will conclude on Monday, Jan. 15 at 11:59 p.m. ET. In the second fan returns, James totaled 1,622,838 votes to pass the Milwaukee Bucks\u2019 Giannis Antetokounmpo (1,480,954) as the top vote-getter in the Eastern Conference and overall. Curry, the Houston Rockets\u2019 James Harden (978,540) and the Oklahoma City Thunder\u2019s Russell Westbrook (791,332) continue to be the top three guards in the West. Durant maintains the top spot in the West frontcourt. The New Orleans Pelicans\u2019 Anthony Davis (664,687) remains in second place, while Golden State\u2019s Draymond Green (616,730) has moved ahead of New Orleans\u2019 DeMarcus Cousins (587,835) for third place. Under a new All-Star Game format that replaces the traditional matchup between conferences, two captains will choose the rosters from the pool of players voted as starters and reserves. The captains will be the All-Star starter from each conference who receives the most fan votes in his conference. The All-Star Game starters will be announced live on TNT on Thursday, Jan. 18 during TNT NBA Tip-Off presented by Autotrader at 6 p.m. ET. The network will unveil the reserves, as selected by NBA head coaches, on Tuesday, Jan. 23 during TNT NBA Tip-Off at 7 p.m. ET. The team rosters will be revealed on Thursday, Jan. 25 in a special one-hour edition of TNT NBA Tip-Off at 7 p.m. ET. That reputation now goes up another notch with a significant milestone. The 11th-year forward scored his 20,000th career point on Wednesday night , an accomplishment only nine players in NBA history did in fewer games. Durant accomplished the feat in 737 games thanks to the 27.1 points per contest he has averaged over that span. When it comes to age, only LeBron James reached the 20,000-point mark sooner. The achievement is significant. Durant is only the 44th player in NBA history to score more than 20,000 points. Only seven retired players who have done so are not yet in the Hall of Fame: Ray Allen, Kobe Bryant, Tom Chambers, Tim Duncan, Kevin Garnett, Antawn Jamison and Paul Pierce. Allen is a nominee for enshrinement this year, while Bryant, Garnett, Duncan and Pierce are considered locks for the honor when they become eligible. Durant\u2019s r\u00e9sum\u00e9 is similarly impressive. In addition to surpassing 20,000 career points, he has also earned the Kia Rookie of the Year Award (2008), All-Star Game MVP (2012), Kia Most Valuable Player Award (2014), The Bill Russell NBA Finals Most Valuable Player Award (2017), an NBA championship (2017), eight All-Star appearances and seven All-NBA honors. This season, Durant is averaging 25.9 points, 7.1 rebounds, 5.3 assists and 2.3 blocks per contest for the league-best Warriors (33-8), who are aiming for their fourth consecutive trip to the NBA Finals. What is wrong with the Cavs\u2019 defense, and how do they fix it?  * * * David Aldridge :  A lot of things. Among the chief ones, though, is that the Cavs don\u2019t seem to be nearly as good with Tristan Thompson and Kevin Love together on the floor. When Thompson was out, and Love was playing center, Cleveland attacked opponents with traps and switched all over the floor, out of necessity as much as innovation \u2014 the undersized Love couldn\u2019t spend all night banging in the paint against guys 20-30 pounds heavier. And, it worked well enough; Cleveland\u2019s defensive rating in November wasn\u2019t great ( 107.2, 21st in the league ), but the Cavs were\/are so productive offensively, with Love having an All-Star caliber start, they just outscored a lot of people. But Iman Shumpert went down at the end of November to arthroscopic knee surgery , and Thompson\u2019s return in mid-December has not helped; Cleveland defensive rating fell significantly in December ( 109.7, 26th ). Thompson has just one double-figure rebounding game in the 12 games since his return, and he\u2019s blocked just three shots overall since coming back. There\u2019s a reason you keep hearing Cleveland linked via the rumor mill to DeAndre Jordan. It\u2019s not all Thompson or Shumpert, but there\u2019s something not right there. Steve Aschburner : It would be nice if the Cavaliers could just go into the gym and strategize their way to stingier defense. See, there\u2019s so much to which they must attend. This is bigger than tweaks. There\u2019s energy missing, there\u2019s effort missing and there\u2019s hunger missing from Cleveland\u2019s game (roster?) now. Adding Isaiah Thomas was an offensive boost but a defensive step back. Nothing is going to bail these guys out short of being scared straight, snapping out of the \u201ctoo cool for the regular season\u201d malaise and realizing bad habits are hard to shake. Shaun Powell :  The players with the best defensive reputations have slipped a bit (Tristan Thompson, J.R. Smith and especially Jae Crowder), making the Cavs more vulnerable than expected. Still, I continue to believe the Cavs, like most of the very best contenders, just want to get through the season. It\u2019s the Boredom Factor. John Schuhmann : What\u2019s wrong? It\u2019s the regular season. How do they fix it? Get to the playoffs. Last season taught us that LeBron James and his team have the ability to flip the switch on that end of the floor. After ranking 29th defensively after the All-Star break and 13th in the first round , they defended well against the Toronto Raptors and Boston Celtics over the next nine games. Of course, in The Finals, they had no chance defending the best offense we\u2019ve ever seen, and the bad habits ( laziness in transition and miscommunication on switches ) that had built up made that more difficult. So yeah, they needed to start fixing things three months ago. Good (and bad) defense starts in transition, where only the Rockets have allowed their opponents to get a greater percentage of their possessions than the Cavs have (16.1 percent) . The Cavs also don\u2019t force their opponents to take inefficient shots; Only Oklahoma City and the Lakers have allowed their opponents to take a greater percentage of their shots from the restricted area or 3-point range, and the 11.9 3-pointers per game the Cavs have allowed would be the second most in NBA history (behind only the 12.1 the Kings are allowing). When the team you\u2019ve got your sights on is the Golden State Warriors, that stat is one that should scare you. But that\u2019s not who they are anymore. Once they decided they\u2019d rather beat the Golden State Warriors by playing the Warriors\u2019 way \u2014 rather than try to stop them with a defensive-minded scheme of their own \u2014 the gradual personnel shift and change in focus took over. And now they are stuck in the same way quite a few teams around the league are, trying to play offensively like the Warriors while being unable to match their style on defense for various reasons. So went the gender reveal at Bankers Life Fieldhouse late last month, when Damien Wilkins and his fianc\u00e9e Jasmine Mitchell found out who would be joining them later this year in Indiana. (I am much, much too old to understand how gender reveal parties have become a thing. We didn\u2019t know the sex of our first child until he was born, and we didn\u2019t want to know.) That Wilkins was in Indiana at all was one of the cool stories of this season \u2014 one that came to an end, at least temporarily, when the team requested waivers on him . (Indiana could, conceivably, look at bringing Wilkins back after the trade deadline, one league source said Sunday afternoon.) At 37, Wilkins worked his way back to the NBA after four years away; he\u2019d last played with the 76ers in 2013. But he thought he had more to give, so he\u2019s spent the last four years playing around the world \u2014 China, Venezuela, Puerto Rico \u2014 along with G League stints in Iowa and Greensboro. But Wilkins continued to believe he would be back in the NBA. He worked in the Players Association\u2019s management training program at the Summer League in Las Vegas in July, and the Pacers\u2019 GM, Kevin Pritchard, ran into him during the \u201cspeed dating\u201d portion of the meeting between the teams and the players, who were and are interested in front office careers. The Pacers brought him to camp on a non-guaranteed deal, and he made the team, playing in 19 games. But his biggest role was as a veteran who could help some of the team\u2019s young players through the ups and downs of an NBA season. The only thing about Wilkins getting released from his standpoint may be that at least he\u2019ll get to watch his alma mater, the University of Georgia, play in the college football national championship game tonight against Alabama. The Pacers are playing Milwaukee tonight; Wilkins had been working with people at Bankers Life Fieldhouse to get regular updates during the game. This interview was done Saturday, before the team decided to let Wilkins go. Me: I know Georgia\u2019s a football school; were you tight with any of the football players back when you were there? Damien Wilkins: Back then, it was like an immature competition between us and them. It took me a while to realize that that was just a competition we weren\u2019t going to win as basketball players. It took me leaving to realize that. Guys like D. J. Shockley was there while I was there. Fred Gibson was there. The guy that works on ESPN now, David Pollack. I was there with those guys. They had a good team them. You know, Georgia always represents the SEC well. If you\u2019re a Bulldog, it\u2019s a proud time right now. Me: What\u2019s that feeling like for you? DW: It feels good. Like I said, it\u2019s a proud time to be a Bulldog right now. I\u2019m sure no one went to class this week. Everyone\u2019s already in Atlanta. I think that more than anything is what\u2019s special about it, because they\u2019re playing that game actually at home. To play that game in our home state, that kind of makes it even more special. It\u2019s surreal, I\u2019m sure, for those guys. Kirby Smart has done a great job. He\u2019s completely turned that thing around, man. He\u2019s got it going in a direction even more so than it was before. Not that we had fallen off any at all, but he\u2019s getting some great recruits. People are staying home to play at Georgia. I mean this guy Jake Fromm is unreal, man. It\u2019s hard to believe he\u2019s a true freshman. We\u2019re extremely proud of those guys right now. But we\u2019ve got a tough task ahead of us, because Alabama sure looked like they played with a chip on their shoulder against Clemson. Me: I think JaMychal Green is the only Alabama guy playing in the league right now. You got any bets down with any \u2018Bama guys? DW: One of our video coordinators, one of our video coordinator interns went to \u2018Bama. We haven\u2019t spoken all week. He\u2019s been wearing his \u2018Bama gear; I\u2019ve been wearing my Georgia gear. So we got a little thing going on right now. Me: What has it been like being back in the league this year? DW: It\u2019s been great. I remember seeing you in Vegas (at the Summer League), and we were talking. I can\u2019t really put it into words. The game has changed so much since the last time I was playing in Philly four years ago. I\u2019m fortunate enough to have been playing and keeping my eye and focus on the game. It\u2019s nothing that surprised me, but it\u2019s been great. It\u2019s just been unbelievable. The experience of being back here and helping these young guys, getting the opportunity to play here and there being back on the floor, being in this element has meant everything to me and it\u2019s all I\u2019ve been working for the past four years. So many people told me I was dumb to play in the D League for two years, I was dumb not to go to Europe and go get money over there, I was dumb to think that this would happen again. It\u2019s just a testament to the perseverance that I had that I was not going to give up until there was just no way out. During that time that I\u2019ve been away, no one was telling me there wasn\u2019t a chance. No one that was able to make decisions for me, basically, was saying \u2018no, you don\u2019t have a chance.\u2019 That\u2019s not what I was hearing. So I was going to give it all that I had to be back. And now that I\u2019m back, it\u2019s just unbelievable. Me: So how has the game changed in the last four years? DW: It\u2019s a lot faster, that\u2019s for sure. You can tell that people now are like, in a hurry to get the ball. That\u2019s how it seems. Everyone\u2019s in a hurry to say, okay, go ahead. Literally, go ahead and score so we can get the ball back. That\u2019s exactly what it feels like sometimes. Everyone\u2019s like, we want to score, so do what you gotta do so we can get the ball inbounds so we can score. And the teams that play defense are the teams at the top of the league. It\u2019s not rocket science. I\u2019m surprised everyone hasn\u2019t figured that out yet. You look at the Spurs and Golden State, even with their frantic pace, they still defend well. Boston is at the top of the league in defensive rating. Toronto, all those teams at the top. Houston has a better defensive roster this year, so it\u2019s no surprise that they\u2019re at the top of the league. That\u2019s the changes I see; the game is a lot faster and everyone\u2019s in such a hurry to get baskets, or to get shot attempts. Me: You\u2019re one of the last active guys who actually played in Seattle. What are your memories from there? DW: I was there three years, four years. I had a great time. That\u2019s an unbelievable city, it\u2019s an unbelievable sports town. Great food, great people, great atmosphere to play basketball, and to play any sport. Look at the Seahawks. It\u2019s just a sports town. I miss it there. It\u2019s a shame that a team isn\u2019t there, and those people there, I know they miss having themselves a basketball team, and hopefully one day it\u2019ll happen. I miss it every day. It\u2019s a great place to play. One of my favorite places. I was fortunate enough to be there my first year. That city kind of raised me \u2014 myself and Nick Collison and even KD for the year that he was there. It was just a great time. Me: When Nate told you he wanted you to be a mentor to the young guys, did you accept that role immediately, or was there any part of you that said, \u2018Hold up \u2014 I can still hoop a little?\u2019 DW: He knew that. He said it to me. He said, I know you still can give us something and I\u2019m probably going to need you at some point. I just don\u2019t know if I need you immediately. And he said to keep up, to stay ready, which he knew I was going to do, anyway. He just said, I\u2019m going to need you to help me with these young guys, call upon you. I obviously know you\u2019re going to be ready. I know you can still play. I said I\u2019ve been waiting four years to hear that. I\u2019m here. I understand how long the season is. The foundation of my career was built on being ready when the opportunity came. It wasn\u2019t anything that was new to me. I\u2019m good with that. As long as I can impact and help these guys mature and grow and be more professional, that\u2019s fine with me. At the end of the day, when it\u2019s all said and done, I really do want to work in the NBA. I want to work in a front office. I want to be a front office exec one day. If that means starting from the coaching standpoint and moving up, that\u2019s fine, too. I\u2019m actually learning on the fly how to lead. I have no problem with that at all. It\u2019s been great so far. I just had dinner last night with Myles Turner. He and I sat and talked for a while. He got some things off his chest that\u2019s been kind of bothering him, and I told him how I felt about some things. Hopefully that helped. It\u2019s things like that that a lot of people probably wouldn\u2019t do. I told him, I don\u2019t ever want to have a regret where I didn\u2019t have that conversation with you. I don\u2019t want to leave here and have it be, man, I wish I would have spoken with him. Man, I wish I would have said this or done that. That was a good thing. He said he lacked veteran leadership in the past, and he appreciated that. And that right there made me feel like my purpose is here. This is the job I\u2019m called to do, and I\u2019m going to do it the best that I can. Me: How are the young guys coming along, like Myles and Victor Oladipo and Domantas Sabonis? DW: We\u2019re coming along fine. We\u2019re coming along just like a young team would. We\u2019ve lost five in a row, so young guys respond to things the way you expect young guys to respond. After you lose two, guys\u2019 confidence gets a little low, or you start to see a little slippage as far as our togetherness goes. But that\u2019s with youth, and that\u2019s with maturity. Guys that have been around before like myself understand that damn near every team in the league goes through a spell where they lose a few in a row. But the good teams, the great teams bounce back. We\u2019ve got an opportunity to bounce back\u2026how this league goes, you get a win, it cures everything. We\u2019re one win away from guys being back, getting our heads back right and getting our confidence back right. Me: So, when\u2019s the last time you got either your dad or your uncle out on the floor? DW: Oh, man, it\u2019s been a while. I don\u2019t think either one of them want to get out there with me. They just talk about it. I was just with \u2018Nique in Atlanta, and he was like, \u2018Yeah, man, I can do this. I can go out and get 15 right now.\u2019 I said, \u2018No, you can\u2019t \u2014 not with all the screens these guys are running off of.
